Chapter 2: File naming conventions 1
1 Introduction
This document describes the compilation environment of Mercury. It describes
how to use â€˜ mmcâ€™, the Mercury compiler;
how to use â€˜ mmc --make â€™, a build tool integrated into â€˜ mmcâ€™;
how to use an older build tool, â€˜ mmake â€™, built on top of GNU make;
how to use â€˜ mdbâ€™, the Mercury debugger;
how to use â€˜ mprof â€™, a â€˜ gprof â€™-style profiler for Mercury; and
how to use â€˜ mdprof â€™, a more detailed profiler for Mercury that uses a web interface.
Mercury programs can be compiled to executables via any one of three target languages:
C, Java and C#. The most frequently used target language is C, for two main reasons.
The first is that several important parts of the Mercury system, such as â€˜ mdbâ€™, â€˜mprof â€™ and
â€˜mdprof â€™, are implemented only when targeting C. The second is that targeting C yields
the fastest executables.
2 File naming conventions
Mercury source files must be named â€˜ *.mâ€™. Each Mercury source file should contain a single
Mercury module whose module name should be (although it does not have to be) the same
as the filename without the â€˜ .mâ€™ extension.
The Mercury implementation uses a variety of intermediate files, which are described
below. But all you really need to know is how to name source files. For historical reasons,
the default behaviour is for intermediate files to be created in the current directory, but if
you use the â€˜ --use-subdirs â€™ option to â€˜ mmcâ€™ or â€˜ mmake â€™, all these intermediate files will be
created in a â€˜ Mercury â€™ subdirectory, where you can happily ignore them. Thus you may
wish to skip the rest of this chapter.
In cases where the source file name and module name donâ€™t match, the names for inter-
mediate files are based on the name of the module from which they are derived, not on the
source file name.
Files whose names end in â€˜ .int â€™, â€˜.int2 â€™, â€˜.int0 â€™ and â€˜ .int3 â€™ are interface files; these are
generated automatically by the compiler, using the â€˜ --make-interface â€™ (or â€˜ --make-int â€™),
â€˜--make-private-interface â€™ (or â€˜ --make-priv-int â€™), â€˜ --make-short-interface â€™
(or â€˜ --make-short-int â€™) options. (Invoking the compiler with â€˜ --make-interface â€™
generates both â€˜ .int â€™ and â€˜ .int2 â€™ files.) Files whose names end in â€˜ .opt â€™ are used for
inter-module optimization, and are created using the â€˜ --make-optimization-interface â€™
(or â€˜ --make-opt-int â€™) option. Similarly, files whose name ends in â€˜ .trans_opt â€™
are used for transitive inter-module optimization, and are created using the
â€˜--make-transitive-optimization-interface â€™ (or â€˜ --make-trans-opt â€™) option.
Since the interface of a module changes less often than its implementation, the â€˜ .int â€™,
â€˜.int0 â€™, â€˜.int2 â€™, â€˜.int3 â€™, â€˜.opt â€™, and â€˜ .trans_opt â€™ files will often remain unchanged when
they are rebuilt. To avoid unnecessary recompilations of the clients of the module, the
timestamps on these files are updated only if their contents change. Files with the suffixes
â€˜.date â€™, â€˜.date0 â€™, â€˜.date3 â€™, â€˜.optdate â€™, and â€˜ .trans_opt_date â€™ serve as timestamp files,
Chapter 3: Using the Mercury compiler 2
to be used when deciding whether the interface files need to be regenerated: they each
record the last time when the file or files it represents has last been checked for being up to
date.
Files ending in â€˜ .dâ€™ are automatically generated Makefile fragments which contain the
dependencies for a module. Files ending in â€˜ .dep â€™ are automatically generated Makefile frag-
ments which contain the rules for an entire program. Files ending in â€˜ .dvâ€™ are automatically
generated Makefile fragments which contain variable definitions for an entire program.
As usual, â€˜ .câ€™ files are C source code, and â€˜ .oâ€™ files are object code. In addition, â€˜ .pic_o â€™
files are object code files that contain position-independent code (PIC). â€˜ .lpic_o â€™ files are
object code files that can be linked with shared libraries, but donâ€™t necessarily contain
position-independent code themselves. â€˜ .mhâ€™ and â€˜ .mih â€™ files are C header files generated by
the Mercury compiler. Their non-standard extensions are necessary to avoid conflicts with
system header files.
â€˜.java â€™ files are Java source code, â€˜ .class â€™ files are Java bytecode, and â€˜ .jar â€™ files are
Java archives.
â€˜.csâ€™ files are C# source code, â€˜ .dll â€™ files are library assemblies and, when compiling to
C#, â€˜ .exe â€™ files are process assemblies.
When smart recompilation (see Section 9.5 [Auxiliary output options], page 101 ) is en-
abled, â€˜ .c_date â€™, â€˜.cs_date â€™, â€˜.java_date â€™ and â€˜ .erl_date â€™ files perform the same function
for â€˜.câ€™, â€˜.csâ€™, â€˜.java â€™ and â€˜ .erl â€™ files respectively as e.g. â€˜ .date3 â€™ files do for â€˜ .int3 â€™ files:
when smart recompilation works out that a module does not need to be recompiled, it up-
dates the timestamp of the timestamp file for the target file, but leaves the timestamp of
the target file itself unchanged.
â€˜.used â€™ files contain dependency information for smart recompilation (see Section 9.5
[Auxiliary output options], page 101 ).
3 Using the Mercury compiler
The Mercury compiler is called â€˜ mmcâ€™ (for â€œMelbourne Mercury Compilerâ€). Some of its
options (e.g. â€˜ -câ€™, â€˜-oâ€™, and â€˜ -Iâ€™) have a similar meaning to that in other compilers.
(Note that on Microsoft Windows systems, the name â€˜ mmcâ€™ is also used by the executable
for the Microsoft Management Console. To avoid the name clash on these systems, you can
either invoke the Mercury compiler by the alternative name â€˜ mercury â€™, or adjust your PATH
to ensure that the â€˜ binâ€™ directory precedes the Windows system directory.)
Arguments to â€˜ mmcâ€™ may be either file names (ending in â€˜ .mâ€™), or module names, with â€˜ .â€™
(rather than â€˜ __â€™) as the module qualifier. For a module name such as â€˜ foo.bar.baz â€™, the
compiler will look for the source in the file named â€˜ foo.bar.baz.m â€™. To make the compiler
look for a module in another file, use â€˜ mmc -f sources-files â€™ to generate a mapping from
module name to file name, where sources-files is the list of source files in the directory (see
Section 9.4 [Output options], page 98 ).
Arguments to â€˜ mmcâ€™ may also be in â€˜ @file â€™. The â€˜ @file â€™ argument is replaced with ar-
guments representing the contents of the file. This argument processing is done recursively.
The contents of the â€˜ @file â€™ is split into arguments one per line in the file.
There are two ways to compile a program that consists of a single source file. The first
way, which works only when targeting C, is to issue the command
Chapter 3: Using the Mercury compiler 3
mmc filename .m
The second way, which works regardless of target language, is to issue the command
mmc --make filename
There are also two ways to build programs that consist of more than one source file:
using Mmake (see Chapter 5 [Using Mmake], page 5 ), which only works when targeting C,
or using â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
We strongly recommend that programmers use â€˜ mmc --make â€™ rather than invoking â€˜ mmcâ€™
directly, because â€˜ mmc --make â€™ is generally easier to use and avoids unnecessary recompila-
tion.
When targeting C on systems that do not require an executable file extension, â€˜ mmcâ€™ will
put the executable into a file called â€˜ filename â€™; on systems (such as Windows) that use
â€˜.exe â€™ as the file extension for executables, â€˜ mmcâ€™ will put the executable into a file called
â€˜filename .exe â€™.
When targeting C#, â€˜ mmcâ€™ will generate a process assembly called â€˜ filename .exe â€™. On
Windows, this process assembly can be run directly. On non-Windows systems, â€˜ mmcâ€™ will
also generate a shell script called â€˜ filename â€™ that invokes the CLI execution environment
on the process assembly. (See the file â€˜ README.CSharp â€™ included in the Mercury distribution
for further details.)
When targeting Java, â€˜ mmcâ€™ will package up all of the class files for the executable into
a Java archive (JAR) named â€˜ filename .jar â€™. If you are using the Windows command line
interpreter â€˜ cmd.exe â€™, â€˜mmcâ€™ will generate a batch file called â€˜ filename .bat â€™ that invokes
the program using the Java interpreter. Otherwise, it will generate a shell script called
â€˜filename â€™ that also invokes the program using the Java interpreter.
If you use Mmake or â€˜ mmc --make â€™, then you do not need to understand the details of
how the Mercury implementation goes about building programs. Thus you may wish to
skip the rest of this chapter.
To compile a source file to object code via C without creating an executable, use the
command
mmc -c filename .m
Since targeting C is the default, this tells â€˜ mmcâ€™ to generate C code, and then invoke the
configured C compiler to translate that to object code. â€˜ mmcâ€™ puts the generated C code
into a file called â€˜ module .câ€™ and the generated object code into a file called â€˜ module .oâ€™,
where module is the name of the Mercury module defined in â€˜ filename .mâ€™. If the source
file contains nested modules, then each submodule will get compiled to separate C and
object files.
Before you can compile a module, you must make the interface files for the modules that
it imports (directly or indirectly). You can create the interface files for one or more source
files using the following commands:
mmc --make-short-int filename1 .mfilename2 .m ...
mmc --make-priv-int filename1 .mfilename2 .m ...
mmc --make-int filename1 .mfilename2 .m ...
The first command builds (or rebuilds) the â€˜ .int3 â€™ file of each module contained in the
named source files. The second command builds (or rebuilds) the â€˜ .int0 â€™ file of each module
contained in the named source files. (Note that only modules that have submodules need
Chapter 3: Using the Mercury compiler 4
â€˜.int0 â€™ files.) The third command builds (or rebuilds) both the â€˜ .int â€™ and â€˜ .int2 â€™ file of
each module contained in the named source files.
If you are going to compile with â€˜ --intermodule-optimization â€™ enabled, then you also
need to create the optimization interface files.
mmc --make-opt-int filename1 .mfilename2 .m ...
If you are going to compile with â€˜ --transitive-intermodule-optimization â€™ enabled,
then you also need to create the transitive optimization files.
mmc --make-trans-opt filename1 .mfilename2 .m ...
After you have made all the interface files, one way to create an executable for a multi-
module program is to compile all the modules at the same time using the command
mmc filename1 .mfilename2 .m ...
This will by default put the resulting executable in â€˜ filename1 â€™, though you can use the
â€˜-ofilename â€™ option to specify a different name for the output file, if you so desire.
The other way to create an executable for a multi-module program is to compile each
module separately using â€˜ mmc -c â€™, and then link the resulting object files together. The
linking is a two stage process.
First, you must create and compile an initialization file , which is a C source file containing
calls to automatically generated initialization functions contained in the C code of the
modules of the program:
c2init module1 .cmodule2 .c ... > main-module _init.c,
mgnuc -c main-module _init.c
The â€˜ c2init â€™ command line must contain the name of the C file of every module in
the program. The order of the arguments is not important. The â€˜ mgnuc â€™ command is the
Mercury GNU C compiler; it is a shell script that invokes the configured C compiler with
the options appropriate for compiling the C programs generated by Mercury. (In the early
days of the Mercury project, the configured C compiler was almost always GCC, which is
why the name of the script is what it is, but the script itself will work with clang or MSVC
as well.)
You then link the object code of each module with the object code of the initialization
file to yield the executable:
ml -o main-module module1 .omodule2 .o ... main_module _init.o
â€˜mlâ€™, the Mercury linker, is another shell script that invokes a C compiler with options
appropriate for Mercury, this time for linking. â€˜ mlâ€™ also pipes any error messages from the
linker through â€˜ mdemangle â€™, the Mercury symbol demangler, so that any error messages
refer to predicate and function names from the Mercury source code rather than to the
names used in the intermediate C code.
The above command puts the executable in the file â€˜ main-module â€™. The same command
line without the â€˜ -oâ€™ option would put the executable into the file â€˜ a.out â€™.
â€˜mmcâ€™ and â€˜ mlâ€™ both accept a â€˜ -vâ€™ (verbose) option. You can use that option to see what
is actually going on. For the full set of options of â€˜ mmcâ€™, see Chapter 9 [Invocation], page 89 .
Chapter 5: Using Mmake 5
4 Running programs
Once you have created an executable for a Mercury program, you can go ahead and execute
it. You may however wish to specify certain options to the Mercury runtime system. The
Mercury runtime accepts options via the MERCURY_OPTIONS environment variable. The most
useful of these are the options that set the size of the stacks. (For the full list of available
options, see Chapter 10 [Environment], page 136 .)
When targeting Java or C#, and when targeting high level C (â€˜ mmc
--high-level-code â€™), stack management is the responsibility of the target lan-
guageâ€™s compiler. When targeting low level C (â€˜ mmcâ€™ without â€“high-level code), stack
management is the responsibility of â€˜ mmcâ€™ and of the C runtime system. This backend uses
two stacks, the det stack and the nondet stack. With â€˜ mmc --stack-segments â€™, both of
these stacks will grow and shrink automatically as needed. Without â€˜ --stack-segments â€™,
their size is fixed at program start-up. The default size is 4096k times the word size (in
bytes) for the det stack and 64k times the word size (in bytes) for the nondet stack, but
these can be overridden with the â€˜ --detstack-size â€™ and â€˜ --nondetstack-size â€™ options,
whose arguments are the desired sizes of the det and nondet stacks respectively, in units
of kilobytes. On operating systems that provide the appropriate support, the Mercury
runtime will ensure that stack overflow is trapped by the virtual memory system.
With conservative garbage collection (the default), the heap will start out with a zero
size, and will be dynamically expanded as needed, When not using conservative garbage
collection, the heap has a fixed size like the stacks. The default size is 8Mb times the word
size (in bytes), but this can be overridden with the â€˜ --heap-size â€™ option.
5 Using Mmake
Mmake, short for â€œMercury Makeâ€, is a tool for building Mercury programs. The same
functionality is now provided in â€˜ mmcâ€™ directly by using the â€˜ --make â€™ option:
mmc --make main-module
The usage of Mmake is discouraged, not least because it works only when targeting C.
Mmake is built on top of GNU Make. With Mmake, building even a complicated Mercury
program consisting of a number of modules is as simple as
mmc -f source-files
mmake main-module .depend
mmake main-module
Mmake only recompiles those files that need to be recompiled, based on automatically
generated dependency information. Most of the dependencies are stored in â€˜ .dâ€™ files that
are automatically recomputed every time you recompile, so they are never out-of-date. A
little bit of the dependency information is stored in â€˜ .dep â€™ and â€˜ .dvâ€™ files which are more
expensive to recompute. The â€˜ mmake main-module .depend â€™ command which recreates the
â€˜main-module .dep â€™ and â€˜ main-module .dvâ€™ files needs to be repeated only when you either
add a module to your program or remove a module from it. There is no danger of getting
an inconsistent executable if you forget this step â€” instead you will get a compile or link
error.
Chapter 5: Using Mmake 6
The â€˜ mmc -f â€™ step above is only required if there are any source files for which the file
name does not match the module name. â€˜ mmc -f â€™ generates a file named â€˜ Mercury.modules â€™
containing a mapping from module name to source file. The â€˜ Mercury.modules â€™ file must
be updated when a source file for which the file name does not match the module name is
added to or removed from the directory.
â€˜mmake â€™ allows you to build more than one program in the same directory. Each
program must have its own â€˜ .dep â€™ and â€˜ .dvâ€™ files, and therefore you must run â€˜ mmake
program .depend â€™ for each program. The â€˜ Mercury.modules â€™ file is used for all programs
in the directory.
If there is a file called â€˜ Mmake â€™ or â€˜Mmakefile â€™ in the current directory, Mmake will include
that file in its automatically generated Makefile. The â€˜ Mmake â€™ file can override the default
values of various variables used by Mmakeâ€™s builtin rules, or it can add additional rules,
dependencies, and actions.
Mmakeâ€™s builtin rules are defined by the file â€˜ prefix /lib/mercury/mmake/Mmake.rules â€™
(where prefix is â€˜/usr/local/mercury- version â€™ by default, and version is the version
number, e.g. â€˜ 0.6â€™), as well as the rules and variables in the automatically generated â€˜ .dep â€™
and â€˜ .dvâ€™ files. These rules define the following targets:
â€˜main-module .depend â€™
Creates the files â€˜ main-module .dep â€™ and â€˜ main-module .dvâ€™ from â€˜ main-
module .mâ€™ and the modules it imports. This step must be performed first.
It is also required whenever you wish to change the level of inter-module
optimization performed (see Section 9.10.1 [Overall optimization options],
page 117 ).
â€˜main-module .all_ints â€™
Ensure that the interface files for main-module and its imported modules are
up-to-date. (If the underlying â€˜ make â€™ program does not handle transitive depen-
dencies, this step may be necessary before attempting to make â€˜ main-module â€™ or
â€˜main-module .check â€™; if the underlying â€˜ make â€™ is GNU Make, this step should
not be necessary.)
â€˜main-module .check â€™
Perform semantic checking on main-module and its imported modules. Error
messages are placed in â€˜ .err â€™ files.
â€˜main-module â€™
Compiles and links main-module using the Mercury compiler. Error messages
are placed in â€˜ .err â€™ files.
â€˜libmain-module â€™
Builds a library whose top-level module is main-module . This will build a
static object library, a shared object library (for platforms that support it), and
the necessary interface files. For more information, see Chapter 6 [Libraries],
page 11 .
â€˜libmain-module .install â€™
Builds and installs a library whose top-level module is main-module . This target
will build and install a static object library and (for platforms that support it)
a shared object library, for the default grade and also for the additional grades
Chapter 5: Using Mmake 7
specified in the LIBGRADES variable. It will also build and install the necessary
interface files. The variable INSTALL specifies the name of the command to use
to install each file, by default â€˜ cpâ€™. The variable INSTALL_MKDIR specifies the
command to use to create directories, by default â€˜ mkdir -p â€™.
â€˜main-module .clean â€™
Removes the automatically generated files that contain the compiled code of
the program and the error messages produced by the compiler. Specifically, this
will remove all the â€˜ .câ€™, â€˜.oâ€™, â€˜.pic_o â€™, â€˜.prof â€™, â€˜.used â€™, â€˜.mih â€™, and â€˜ .err â€™ files
belonging to the named main-module or its imported modules. Use this target
whenever you wish to change compilation model (see Section 9.8 [Compilation
model options], page 108 ). This target is also recommended whenever you wish
to change the level of inter-module optimization performed (see Section 9.10.1
[Overall optimization options], page 117 ) in addition to the mandatory main-
module .depend.
â€˜main-module .realclean â€™
Removes all the automatically generated files. In addition to the files
removed by main-module .clean, this removes the â€˜ .int â€™, â€˜.int0 â€™, â€˜.int2 â€™,
â€˜.int3 â€™, â€˜.opt â€™, â€˜.trans_opt â€™, â€˜.date â€™, â€˜.date0 â€™, â€˜.date3 â€™, â€˜.optdate â€™,
â€˜.trans_opt_date â€™, â€˜.mhâ€™ and â€˜ .dâ€™ files belonging to one of the modules of the
program, and also the various possible executables, libraries and dependency
files for the program as a whole â€” â€˜ main-module â€™, â€˜libmain-module .aâ€™,
â€˜libmain-module .soâ€™, â€˜ libmain-module .dylib â€™, â€˜ main-module .init â€™,
â€˜main-module .dep â€™ and â€˜ main-module .dvâ€™.
â€˜clean â€™ This makes â€˜ main-module .clean â€™ for every main-module for which there is a
â€˜main-module .dep â€™ file in the current directory, as well as deleting the profil-
ing files â€˜ Prof.CallPair â€™, â€˜Prof.Counts â€™, â€˜Prof.Decl â€™, â€˜Prof.MemWords â€™ and
â€˜Prof.MemCells â€™.
â€˜realclean â€™
This makes â€˜ main-module .realclean â€™ for every main-module for which there
is a â€˜ main-module .dep â€™ file in the current directory, as well as deleting the
profiling files as per the â€˜ clean â€™ target.
The variables used by the builtin rules (and their default values) are defined in the
file â€˜prefix /lib/mercury/mmake/Mmake.vars â€™, however these may be overridden by user
â€˜Mmake â€™ files. Some of the more useful variables are:
MAIN_TARGET
The name of the default target to create if â€˜ mmake â€™ is invoked with any target
explicitly named on the command line.
MC The executable that invokes the Mercury compiler.
GRADEFLAGS and EXTRA_GRADEFLAGS
Compilation model options (see Section 9.8 [Compilation model options],
page 108 ) to pass to the Mercury compiler, linker, and other tools (in
particular mmc,mgnuc ,ml, and c2init ).
Chapter 5: Using Mmake 8
MCFLAGS and EXTRA_MCFLAGS
Options to pass to the Mercury compiler. (Note that compilation model options
should be specified in GRADEFLAGS , not in MCFLAGS .)
MGNUC The executable that invokes the C compiler.
MGNUCFLAGS and EXTRA_MGNUCFLAGS
Options to pass to the mgnuc script.
CFLAGS and EXTRA_CFLAGS
Options to pass to the C compiler.
JAVACFLAGS and EXTRA_JAVACFLAGS
Options to pass to the Java compiler (if you are using it).
ML The executable that invokes the linker.
LINKAGE Can be set to either â€˜ shared â€™ to link with shared libraries, or â€˜ static â€™ to always
link statically. The default is â€˜ shared â€™. This variable only has an effect with
â€˜mmc --make â€™.
MERCURY_LINKAGE
Can be set to either â€˜ shared â€™ to link with shared Mercury libraries, or â€˜ static â€™
to always link with the static versions of Mercury libraries. The default is
system dependent. This variable only has an effect with â€˜ mmc --make â€™. See
Section 6.2.2 [Using installed libraries with mmc â€“make], page 12 .
MLFLAGS and EXTRA_MLFLAGS
Options to pass to the ml and c2init scripts. (Note that compilation model
options should be specified in GRADEFLAGS , not in MLFLAGS .) These variables
have no effect with â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
LDFLAGS and EXTRA_LDFLAGS
Options to pass to the command used by the ml script to link executables (use
ml --print-link-command to find out what command is used, usually the C
compiler).
LD_LIBFLAGS and EXTRA_LD_LIBFLAGS
Options to pass to the command used to by the ml script to link shared
libraries (use ml --print-shared-lib-link-command to find out what com-
mand is used, usually the C compiler or the system linker, depending on the
platform).
MLLIBS and EXTRA_MLLIBS
A list of â€˜ -lâ€™ options specifying libraries used by the program (or library) that
you are building. See Section 6.3.3 [Using libraries with Mmake], page 15 . See
Section 6.2.2 [Using installed libraries with mmc â€“make], page 12 .
MLOBJS and EXTRA_MLOBJS
A list of extra object files or archives to link into the program or library that
you are building.
C2INITFLAGS and EXTRA_C2INITFLAGS
Options to pass to the linker and the c2init program. C2INITFLAGS andEXTRA_
C2INITFLAGS are obsolete synonyms for MLFLAGS andEXTRA_MLFLAGS (mland
Chapter 5: Using Mmake 9
c2init take the same set of options). (Note that compilation model options
and extra files to be processed by c2init should not be specified in C2INITFLAGS
â€” they should be specified in GRADEFLAGS andC2INITARGS , respectively.)
C2INITARGS and EXTRA_C2INITARGS
Extra files to be processed by c2init. These variables should not be used for
specifying flags to c2init (those should be specified in MLFLAGS ) since they are
also used to derive extra dependency information.
EXTRA_LIBRARIES
A list of extra Mercury libraries to link into any programs or libraries that
you are building. Libraries should be specified using their base name; that
is, without any â€˜ libâ€™ prefix or extension. For example, the library including
the files â€˜ libfoo.a â€™ and â€˜ foo.init â€™ would be referred to as just â€˜ fooâ€™. See
Section 6.3.3 [Using libraries with Mmake], page 15 . See Section 6.2.2 [Using
installed libraries with mmc â€“make], page 12 .
EXTRA_LIB_DIRS
A list of extra Mercury library directory hierarchies to search when looking for
extra libraries. See Section 6.3.3 [Using libraries with Mmake], page 15 . See
Section 6.2.2 [Using installed libraries with mmc â€“make], page 12 .
INSTALL_PREFIX
The path to the root of the directory hierarchy where the libraries, etc. you are
building should be installed. The default is to install in the same location as
the Mercury compiler being used to do the install.
INSTALL The command used to install each file in a library. The command should take
a list of files to install and the location to install them. The default command
is â€˜cpâ€™.
INSTALL_MKDIR
The command used to create each directory in the directory hierarchy where
the libraries are to be installed. The default command is â€˜ mkdir -p â€™.
LIBGRADES
A list of additional grades which should be built when installing libraries. The
default is to install the Mercury compilerâ€™s default set of grades. Note that
this may not be the set of grades in which the standard libraries were actually
installed. Note also that any GRADEFLAGS settings will also be applied when the
library is built in each of the listed grades, so you may not get what you expect
if those options are not subsumed by each of the grades listed.
LIB_LINKAGES
A list of linkage styles (â€˜ shared â€™ or â€˜ static â€™) for which libraries should be built
and installed. The default is to install libraries for both static and shared
linking. This variable only has an effect with â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
Other variables also exist â€” see â€˜ prefix /lib/mercury/mmake/Mmake.vars â€™ for a com-
plete list.
If you wish to temporarily change the flags passed to an executable, rather than setting
the various â€˜ FLAGS â€™ variables directly, you can set an â€˜ EXTRA_ â€™ variable. This is particularly
Chapter 5: Using Mmake 10
intended for use where a shell script needs to call mmake and add an extra parameter,
without interfering with the flag settings in the â€˜ Mmakefile â€™.
For each of the variables for which there is version with an â€˜ EXTRA_ â€™ prefix, there is also
a version with an â€˜ ALL_ â€™ prefix that is defined to include both the ordinary and the â€˜ EXTRA_ â€™
version. If you wish to usethe values any of these variables in your Mmakefile (as opposed
tosetting the values), then you should use the â€˜ ALL_ â€™ version.
It is also possible to override these variables on a per-file basis. For example, if you
have a module called say â€˜ bad_style.m â€™ which triggers lots of compiler warnings, and you
want to disable the warnings just for that file, but keep them for all the other modules,
then you can override MCFLAGS just for that file. This is done by setting the variable
â€˜MCFLAGS-bad_style â€™, as shown here:
MCFLAGS-bad_style = --inhibit-warnings
Mmake has a few options, including â€˜ --use-subdirs â€™, â€˜ --use-mmc-make â€™,
â€˜--save-makefile â€™, â€˜--verbose â€™, and â€˜ --no-warn-undefined-vars â€™. For details about
these options, see the man page or type â€˜ mmake --help â€™.
Finally, since Mmake is built on top of GNU Make, you can also make use of the features
and options supported by the underlying Make. In particular, GNU Make has support for
running jobs in parallel, which is very useful if you have a machine with more than one
CPU.
As an alternative to Mmake, the Mercury compiler now contains a significant part of
the functionality of Mmake, using â€˜ mmcâ€™â€™s â€˜--make â€™ option.
The advantages of the â€˜ mmc --make â€™ over Mmake are that there is no â€˜ mmake depend â€™
step and the dependencies are more accurate.
Note that â€˜ --use-subdirs â€™ is automatically enabled if you specify â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
The Mmake variables above can be used by â€˜ mmc --make â€™ if they are set in a file called
â€˜Mercury.options â€™. The â€˜ Mercury.options â€™ file has the same syntax as an Mmakefile,
but only variable assignments and â€˜ include â€™ directives are allowed. All variables in
â€˜Mercury.options â€™ are treated as if they are assigned using â€˜ :=â€™. Variables may also be
set in the environment, overriding settings in options files.
â€˜mmc --make â€™ can be used in conjunction with Mmake. This is useful for projects which
include source code written in languages other than Mercury. The â€˜ --use-mmc-make â€™
Mmake option disables Mmakeâ€™s Mercury-specific rules. Mmake will then process source
files written in other languages, but all Mercury compilation will be done by â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
The following variables can be set in the Mmakefile to control the use of â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
MERCURY_MAIN_MODULES
The top-level modules of the programs or libraries being built in the directory.
This must be set to tell Mmake to use â€˜ mmc --make â€™ to rebuild the targets for
the main modules even if those files already exist.
MC_BUILD_FILES
Other files which should be built with â€˜ mmc --make â€™. This should only be nec-
essary for header files generated by the Mercury compiler which are included
by the userâ€™s C source files.
MC_MAKE_FLAGS and EXTRA_MC_MAKE_FLAGS
Options to pass to the Mercury compiler only when using â€˜ mmc --make â€™.
Chapter 6: Libraries 11
The following variables can also appear in options files but are only supported by
â€˜mmc --make â€™.
GCC_FLAGS
Options to pass to the C compiler, but only if the C compiler is GCC. If the C
compiler is not GCC then this variable is ignored. These options will be passed
after any options given by the â€˜ CFLAGS â€™ variable.
CLANG_FLAGS
Options to pass to the C compiler, but only if the C compiler is clang. If the C
compiler is not clang then this variable is ignored. These options will be passed
after any options given by the â€˜ CFLAGS â€™ variable.
MSVC_FLAGS
Options to pass to the C compiler, but only if the C compiler is Microsoft Visual
C. If the C compiler is not Visual C then this variable is ignored. These options
will be passed after any options given by the â€˜ CFLAGS â€™ variable.
6 Libraries
Often you will want to use a particular set of Mercury modules in more than one pro-
gram. The Mercury implementation includes support for developing libraries, i.e. sets of
Mercury modules intended for reuse. It allows separate compilation of libraries and, on
many platforms, it supports shared object libraries.
6.1 Writing libraries
A Mercury library is identified by a top-level module, which should contain all of the modules
in that library as submodules. It may be as simple as this â€˜ mypackage.m â€™ file:
:- module mypackage.
:- interface.
:- include_module foo.
:- include_module bar.
:- include_module baz.
This defines a module â€˜ mypackage â€™ containing submodules â€˜ mypackage.foo â€™,
â€˜mypackage.bar â€™, and â€˜ mypackage.baz â€™.
It is also possible to build libraries of unrelated modules, so long as the top-level module
imports all the necessary modules. For example:
:- module blah.
:- implementation.
:- import_module fee.
:- import_module fie.
:- import_module foe.
:- import_module fum.
This example defines a module â€˜ blah â€™, which has no functionality of its own, and which
is just used for grouping the unrelated modules â€˜ feeâ€™, â€˜fieâ€™, â€˜foeâ€™, and â€˜ fumâ€™. To avoid
a warning about the interface of this module being empty, this module would have to be
Chapter 6: Libraries 12
compiled with â€˜ --no-warn-nothing-exported â€™. Alternatively, the library could of course
just export something, such as a predicate that returns its version number.
Generally it is better style for each library to consist of a single module which encapsu-
lates its submodules, as in the first example, rather than just a group of unrelated modules,
as in the second example.
6.2 Building with mmc â€“make
6.2.1 Building and installing libraries with mmc â€“make
To build a library from the source â€˜ mypackage.m â€™ (and other included modules), run â€˜ mmcâ€™
with the following arguments:
mmc --make libmypackage
â€˜mmcâ€™ will create static (non-shared) object libraries and, on most platforms, shared object
libraries; however, we do not yet support the creation of dynamic link libraries (DLLs) on
Windows. Use the â€˜ mmcâ€™ option â€˜ --lib-linkage â€™ to specify which versions of the library
should be created: â€˜ shared â€™ or â€˜ static â€™. The â€˜ --lib-linkage â€™ option can be specified
multiple times. In our example, the files â€˜ libmypackage.a â€™ and â€˜ libmypackage.so â€™ should
appear in the current directory. (On macOS â€˜ libmypackage.dylib â€™ will appear instead of
â€˜libmypackage.so â€™.)
Other programs can more easily use a library that is installed. To install the library,
issue the following command:
mmc --make --install-prefix <dir> libmypackage.install
â€˜mmcâ€™ will create the directory â€˜ <dir>/lib/mercury â€™ and install the library there. The
library will be compiled in all valid grades and with all interface files. Because several grades
are usually compiled, installing the library can be a lengthy process. You can specify the
set of installed grades using the option â€˜ --no-libgrade â€™ followed by â€˜ --libgrade <grade> â€™
for all grades you wish to install.
If no â€˜ --install-prefix <dir> â€™ is specified, the library will be installed in the standard
location, next to the Mercury standard library.
6.2.2 Using installed libraries with mmc â€“make
Once a library is installed, it can be used by running â€˜ mmcâ€™ with the following options:
mmc ... --ml mypackage ... --ml myotherlib ... --ml my_yet_another_lib ...
If a library was installed in a different place (using â€˜ --install-prefix <dir> â€™), you will
also need to add this option:
mmc ... --mld <dir>/lib/mercury ...
Note that â€˜ /lib/mercury â€™ has to be added to the searched path. The â€˜ --mld â€™ option can
be used several times to add more directories to the library search path.
You can also specify whether to link executables with the shared or static versions of
Mercury libraries using â€˜ --mercury-linkage shared â€™ or â€˜ --mercury-linkage static â€™.
Chapter 6: Libraries 13
6.2.3 Using non-installed libraries with mmc â€“make
Suppose the user wants to link against library â€˜ mypackage â€™ without installing the library.
The source of the library is stored in the directory â€˜ <dir> â€™ and that the library has been
properly built using â€˜ mmc --make libmypackage â€™. To link against the library, the following
options have to be added to â€˜ mmcâ€™:
mmc ... --search-lib-files-dir <dir> \
--init-file <dir>/mypackage.init \
--link-object <dir>/libmypackage.a \
...
Note that the option â€˜ --ml â€™ is not used.
You need to make sure the library â€˜ libmypackage.a â€™ and the main program were com-
piled in the same grade.
If you need to experiment with more grades, be sure to build the library in all the grades
(building several times using â€˜ mmc --grade <grade> --make libmypackage â€™) and use the
â€˜libmypackage.a â€™ that is compatible with your main programâ€™s grade:
mmc ... --use-grade-subdirs \
--grade <grade> \
--search-lib-files-dir <dir> \
--init-file <dir>/mypackage.init \
--link-object <dir>/Mercury/<grade>/*/Mercury/lib/libmypackage.a \
...
6.3 Building with Mmake
6.3.1 Building libraries with Mmake
Generally Mmake will do most of the work of building libraries automatically. Here is a
sample Mmakefile for creating a library.
MAIN_TARGET = libmypackage
depend: mypackage.depend
The Mmake target â€˜ libfooâ€™ is a builtin target for creating a library whose top-level
module is â€˜ foo.mâ€™. The automatically generated Mmake rules for the target â€˜ libfooâ€™ will
create all the files needed to use the library. (You will need to run â€˜ mmake foo.depend â€™ first
to generate the module dependency information.)
Mmake will create static (non-shared) object libraries and, on most platforms, shared
object libraries; however, we do not yet support the creation of dynamic link libraries (DLLs)
on Windows. Static libraries are created using the standard tools arand ranlib . Shared
libraries are created using the â€˜ --make-shared-lib â€™ option to â€˜ mlâ€™. The automatically
generated Make rules for â€˜ libmypackage â€™ will look something like this:
libmypackage: libmypackage.a libmypackage.so \
$(mypackage.ints) $(mypackage.int3s) \
$(mypackage.opts) $(mypackage.trans_opts) mypackage.init
libmypackage.a: $(mypackage.os)
rm -f libmypackage.a
Chapter 6: Libraries 14
$(AR) $(ARFLAGS) libmypackage.a $(mypackage.os) $(MLOBJS)
$(RANLIB) $(RANLIBFLAGS) mypackage.a
libmypackage.so: $(mypackage.pic_os)
$(ML) $(MLFLAGS) --make-shared-lib -o libmypackage.so \
$(mypackage.pic_os) $(MLPICOBJS) $(MLLIBS)
libmypackage.init:
...
clean:
rm -f libmypackage.a libmypackage.so
If necessary, you can override the default definitions of the variables such as â€˜ MLâ€™,
â€˜MLFLAGS â€™, â€˜MLPICOBJS â€™, and â€˜ MLLIBS â€™ to customize the way shared libraries are built. Simi-
larly â€˜ ARâ€™, â€˜ARFLAGS â€™, â€˜MLOBJS â€™, â€˜RANLIB â€™, and â€˜ RANLIBFLAGS â€™ control the way static libraries
are built. (The â€˜ MLOBJS â€™ variable is supposed to contain a list of additional object files to
link into the library, while the â€˜ MLLIBS â€™ variable should contain a list of â€˜ -lâ€™ options naming
other libraries used by this library. â€˜ MLPICOBJS â€™ is described below.)
Note that to use a library, as well as the shared or static object library, you also need
the interface files. That is why the â€˜ libmypackage â€™ target builds â€˜ $(mypackage.ints) â€™ and
â€˜$(mypackage.int3s) â€™. If the people using the library are going to use intermodule opti-
mization, you will also need the intermodule optimization interfaces. The â€˜ libmypackage â€™
target will build â€˜ $(mypackage.opts) â€™ if â€˜ --intermodule-optimization â€™ is specified in
your â€˜ MCFLAGS â€™ variable (this is recommended). Similarly, if the people using the library
are going to use transitive intermodule optimization, you will also need the transitive
intermodule optimization interfaces (â€˜ $(mypackage.trans_opt) â€™). These will be built if
â€˜--trans-intermod-opt â€™ is specified in your â€˜ MCFLAGS â€™ variable.
In addition, with certain compilation grades, programs will need to execute some startup
code to initialize the library; the â€˜ mypackage.init â€™ file contains information about initial-
ization code for the library. The â€˜ libmypackage â€™ target will build this file.
On some platforms, shared objects must be created using position independent code
(PIC), which requires passing some special options to the C compiler. On these plat-
forms, Mmake will create â€˜ .pic_o â€™ files, and â€˜ $(mypackage.pic_os) â€™ will contain a list
of the â€˜ .pic_o â€™ files for the library whose top-level module is â€˜ mypackage â€™. In addition,
â€˜$(MLPICOBJS) â€™ will be set to â€˜ $MLOBJS â€™ with all occurrences of â€˜ .oâ€™ replaced with â€˜ .pic_o â€™.
On other platforms, position independent code is the default, so â€˜ $(mypackage.pic_os) â€™
will just be the same as â€˜ $(mypackage.os) â€™, which contains a list of the â€˜ .oâ€™ files for that
module, and â€˜ $(MLPICOBJS) â€™ will be the same as â€˜ $(MLOBJS) â€™.
6.3.2 Installing libraries with Mmake
â€˜mmake â€™ has support for alternative library directory hierarchies. These have the same struc-
ture as the â€˜ prefix /lib/mercury â€™ tree, including the different subdirectories for different
grades and different machine architectures.
In order to support the installation of a library into such a tree, you simply need to
specify (e.g. in your â€˜ Mmakefile â€™) the path prefix and the list of grades to install:
Chapter 6: Libraries 15
INSTALL_PREFIX = /my/install/dir
LIBGRADES = asm_fast asm_fast.gc.tr.debug
This specifies that libraries should be installed in â€˜ /my/install/dir/lib/mercury â€™, in
the default grade plus â€˜ asm_fast â€™ and â€˜ asm_fast.gc.tr.debug â€™. If â€˜ INSTALL_PREFIX â€™ is
not specified, â€˜ mmake â€™ will attempt to install the library in the same place as the standard
Mercury libraries. If â€˜ LIBGRADES â€™ is not specified, â€˜ mmake â€™ will use the Mercury compilerâ€™s
default set of grades, which may or may not correspond to the actual set of grades in which
the standard Mercury libraries were installed.
To actually install a library â€˜ libfooâ€™, use the â€˜ mmake â€™ target â€˜ libfoo.install â€™. This
also installs all the needed interface files, and (if intermodule optimisation is enabled) the
relevant intermodule optimisation files.
One can override the list of grades to install for a given library â€˜ libfooâ€™ by setting the
â€˜LIBGRADES- fooâ€™ variable, or add to it by setting â€˜ EXTRA_LIBGRADES- fooâ€™.
The command used to install each file is specified by â€˜ INSTALL â€™. If â€˜ INSTALL â€™ is not
specified, â€˜ cpâ€™ will be used.
The command used to create directories is specified by â€˜ INSTALL_MKDIR â€™. If
â€˜INSTALL_MKDIR â€™ is not specified, â€˜ mkdir -p â€™ will be used.
Note that currently it is not possible to set the installation prefix on a library-by-library
basis.
6.3.3 Using libraries with Mmake
Once a library is installed, using it is easy. Suppose the user wishes to use the library
â€˜mypackage â€™ (installed in the tree rooted at â€˜ /some/directory/mypackage â€™) and the library
â€˜myotherlib â€™ (installed in the tree rooted at â€˜ /some/directory/myotherlib â€™). The user
need only set the following Mmake variables:
EXTRA_LIB_DIRS = /some/directory/mypackage/lib/mercury \
/some/directory/myotherlib/lib/mercury
EXTRA_LIBRARIES = mypackage myotherlib
When using â€˜ --intermodule-optimization â€™ with a library which uses the C interface,
it may be necessary to add â€˜ -Iâ€™ options to â€˜ MGNUCFLAGS â€™ so that the C compiler can find
any header files used by the libraryâ€™s C code.
Mmake will ensure that the appropriate directories are searched for the relevant interface
files, module initialisation files, compiled libraries, etc.
Beware that the directory name that you must use in â€˜ EXTRA_LIB_DIRS â€™ or as the ar-
gument of the â€˜ --mld â€™ option is not quite the same as the name that was specified in the
â€˜INSTALL_PREFIX â€™ when the library was installed â€” the name needs to have â€˜ /lib/mercury â€™
appended.
One can specify extra libraries to be used on a program-by-program basis. For instance,
if the program â€˜ fooâ€™ also uses the library â€˜ mylib4foo â€™, but the other programs governed by
the Mmakefile donâ€™t, then one can declare:
EXTRA_LIBRARIES-foo = mylib4foo

6.4 Libraries and the Java grade
To create or install a library in the Java grade, specify that you want to use the Java grade
and use â€˜ mmc --make â€™. Mmake does notsupport Java targets.
